<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Christopher Columbus</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Italian explorer, navigator, and colonizer (1451–1506)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Cristoforo Colombo" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus_(disambiguation)" title="Christopher Columbus (disambiguation)">Christopher Columbus (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Cristoforo_Colombo_(disambiguation)" title="Cristoforo Colombo (disambiguation)">Cristoforo Colombo (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Admiral of the Ocean Sea" redirects here. For his descendant, the current holder of the title, see <a href="/wiki/Crist%C3%B3bal_Col%C3%B3n_de_Carvajal,_18th_Duke_of_Veragua" title="Cristóbal Colón de Carvajal, 18th Duke of Veragua">Cristóbal Colón de Carvajal, 18th Duke of Veragua</a>. For other holders of this title, see <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Veragua" title="Duke of Veragua">Duke of Veragua</a>.</div>

<p><b>Christopher Columbus</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> born between 25 August and 31 October 1451, died 20 May 1506) was an Italian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[b]</a></sup> explorer and navigator who completed <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">four Spanish-based voyages across the Atlantic Ocean</a> sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs_of_Spain" title="Catholic Monarchs of Spain">Catholic Monarchs of Spain</a>, opening the way for the widespread European <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">exploration</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Colonization of the Americas">colonization of the Americas</a>. His expeditions were the first known European contact with the Caribbean, Central America, and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>.
</p><p>The name <i>Christopher Columbus</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">anglicisation</a> of the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Christophorus Columbus</i></span>. Scholars generally agree that Columbus was born in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Republic of Genoa</a> and spoke a dialect of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligurian_(Romance_language)" title="Ligurian (Romance language)">Ligurian</a> as his first language. He went to sea at a young age and travelled widely, as far north as the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> and as far south as what is now <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>. He married Portuguese noblewoman <a href="/wiki/Filipa_Moniz_Perestrelo" title="Filipa Moniz Perestrelo">Filipa Moniz Perestrelo</a>, who bore his son Diego, and was based in Lisbon for several years. He later took a Castilian mistress, <a href="/wiki/Beatriz_Enr%C3%ADquez_de_Arana" title="Beatriz Enríquez de Arana">Beatriz Enríquez de Arana</a>, who bore his son, Fernando (also given as Hernando).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernández-Armesto2010_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernández-Armesto2010-8">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taviani201624_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taviani201624-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992126_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992126-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Largely self-educated, Columbus was widely read in geography, astronomy, and history. He developed a plan to seek a western sea passage to the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a>, hoping to profit from the lucrative <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Granada_War" title="Granada War">Granada War</a>, and following Columbus's persistent lobbying in multiple kingdoms, the Catholic Monarchs <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Queen Isabella I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">King Ferdinand II</a> agreed to sponsor a journey west. Columbus left Castile in August 1492 with three ships and made landfall in the Americas on 12 October, ending the period of human habitation in the Americas now referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian era</a>. His landing place was an island in <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">the Bahamas</a>, known by its native inhabitants as <a href="/wiki/Guanahani" title="Guanahani">Guanahani</a>. He subsequently visited the islands now known as <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>, establishing <a href="/wiki/La_Navidad" title="La Navidad">a colony</a> in what is now Haiti. Columbus returned to Castile in early 1493, bringing a number of captured natives with him. <a href="/wiki/Columbus%27s_letter_on_the_first_voyage" title="Columbus's letter on the first voyage">Word of his voyage</a> soon spread throughout Europe.
</p><p>Columbus made three further voyages to the Americas, exploring the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Antilles" title="Lesser Antilles">Lesser Antilles</a> in 1493, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad" title="Trinidad">Trinidad</a> and the northern coast of South America in 1498, and the eastern coast of Central America in 1502. Many of the names he gave to geographical features, particularly islands, are still in use. He also gave the name <i>indios</i> ("Indians") to the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous peoples</a> he encountered. The extent to which he was aware that the Americas were a wholly separate landmass is uncertain; he never clearly renounced his belief that he had reached the Far East. As a colonial governor, Columbus was accused by his contemporaries of significant brutality and was soon removed from the post. Columbus's strained relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Crown of Castile</a> and its appointed colonial administrators in America led to his arrest and removal from Hispaniola in 1500, and later to <a href="/wiki/Pleitos_colombinos" title="Pleitos colombinos">protracted litigation</a> over the perquisites that he and his heirs claimed were owed to them by the crown.
</p><p>Columbus's expeditions inaugurated a period of exploration, conquest, and colonization that lasted for centuries, helping create the modern <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>. The transfers between the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> that followed his first voyage are known as the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a>. Columbus was widely celebrated in the centuries after his death, but public perception has fractured in the 21st century as scholars have given greater attention to the harms committed under his governance, particularly the beginning of the depopulation of Hispaniola's indigenous <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno" title="Taíno">Taínos</a> caused by mistreatment and Old World diseases, as well as by that people's <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">enslavement</a>. Proponents of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Legend_(Spain)" title="Black Legend (Spain)">Black Legend</a> theory of <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a> claim that Columbus has been unfairly maligned as part of a wider <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Catholic_sentiment" title="Anti-Catholic sentiment">anti-Catholic sentiment</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_places_named_for_Christopher_Columbus" title="List of places named for Christopher Columbus">places</a> in the Western Hemisphere bear <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(personification)" title="Columbia (personification)">his name</a>, including the country of <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information on Columbus's birthplace and family background: <a href="/wiki/Origin_theories_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Origin theories of Christopher Columbus">Origin theories of Christopher Columbus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Casa_di_Colombo_Genova_foto_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Casa_di_Colombo_Genova_foto_2.jpg/220px-Casa_di_Colombo_Genova_foto_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Casa_di_Colombo_Genova_foto_2.jpg/330px-Casa_di_Colombo_Genova_foto_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Casa_di_Colombo_Genova_foto_2.jpg/440px-Casa_di_Colombo_Genova_foto_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Casa_di_Colombo_Genova_foto_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus_House" title="Christopher Columbus House">Christopher Columbus House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, Italy, an 18th-century reconstruction of the house in which Columbus grew up. The original was likely destroyed during the 1684 <a href="/wiki/Bombardment_of_Genoa" title="Bombardment of Genoa">bombardment of Genoa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Columbus's early life is obscure, but scholars believe he was born in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Republic of Genoa</a> between 25 August and 31 October 1451.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards2014_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards2014-13">[11]</a></sup>  His father was <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Colombo" title="Domenico Colombo">Domenico Colombo</a>, a wool weaver who worked in Genoa and <a href="/wiki/Savona" title="Savona">Savona</a> and who also owned a cheese stand at which young Christopher worked as a helper. His mother was <a href="/wiki/Susanna_Fontanarossa" title="Susanna Fontanarossa">Susanna Fontanarossa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199291_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199291-14">[12]</a></sup> He had three brothers—<a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Columbus" title="Bartholomew Columbus">Bartolomeo</a>, Giovanni Pellegrino, and Giacomo (also called Diego)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diego_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Diego-2">[2]</a></sup>—as well as a sister named Bianchinetta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen201156_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen201156-15">[13]</a></sup> His brother Bartolomeo ran a <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a> workshop in <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> for at least part of his adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King2021_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-King2021-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>His native language is presumed to have been a <a href="/wiki/Genoese_dialect" title="Genoese dialect">Genoese dialect</a> although Columbus probably never wrote in that language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199296_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199296-17">[15]</a></sup> His name in the 16th-century Genoese language was <i>Cristoffa Corombo</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galante2022_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galante2022-18">[16]</a></sup> (<small>Ligurian pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" lang="lij-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Ligurian" title="Help:IPA/Ligurian">[kriˈʃtɔffa kuˈɹuŋbu]</a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> His name in <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> is <i>Cristoforo Colombo</i>, and in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> <i>Cristóbal Colón</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SánchezGuruléBroughton1990_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-SánchezGuruléBroughton1990-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedini2016_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bedini2016-21">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In one of his writings, he says he went to sea at the age of fourteen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199296_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199296-17">[15]</a></sup> In 1470, the Colombo family moved to <a href="/wiki/Savona" title="Savona">Savona</a>, where Domenico took over a tavern. Some modern authors have argued that he was not from Genoa but, instead, from the <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">Aragon</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilgus1973_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilgus1973-22">[20]</a></sup> or from <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> These competing hypotheses generally have been discounted by mainstream scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson19973_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson19973-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199285_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199285-25">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1473, Columbus began his apprenticeship as business agent for the wealthy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinola_family" title="Spinola family">Spinola</a>, Centurione, and Di Negro families of Genoa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lyon1992_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lyon1992-26">[24]</a></sup> Later, he made a trip to <a href="/wiki/Chios" title="Chios">Chios</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> island then ruled by Genoa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199293_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips199293-27">[25]</a></sup> In May 1476, he took part in an armed convoy sent by Genoa to carry valuable cargo to northern Europe. He probably visited <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>, England,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vigneras2016_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vigneras2016-28">[26]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Galway" title="Galway">Galway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UrelandClarkson2011_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-UrelandClarkson2011-29">[27]</a></sup> where he may have visited <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Nicholas_Collegiate_Church" title="St. Nicholas Collegiate Church">St. Nicholas Collegiate Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graves1949_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graves1949-30">[28]</a></sup> It has been speculated that he had also gone to <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> in 1477, although many scholars doubt it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enterline2003_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enterline2003-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PaolucciPaolucci1992_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-PaolucciPaolucci1992-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolodny2012_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kolodny2012-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34">[32]</a></sup> It is known that in the autumn of 1477, he sailed on a Portuguese ship from Galway to Lisbon, where he found his brother Bartolomeo, and they continued trading for the Centurione family. Columbus based himself in Lisbon from 1477 to 1485. In 1478, the Centuriones sent Columbus on a sugar-buying trip to Madeira.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernández-Armesto1991_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernández-Armesto1991-35">[33]</a></sup> He married <a href="/wiki/Filipa_Moniz_Perestrelo" title="Filipa Moniz Perestrelo">Felipa Perestrello e Moniz</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Perestrello" title="Bartolomeu Perestrello">Bartolomeu Perestrello</a>, a Portuguese nobleman of <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombard</a> origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FreitasManey1893_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FreitasManey1893-36">[34]</a></sup> who had been the <a href="/wiki/Donatary_captain" title="Donatary captain">donatary captain</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porto_Santo" title="Porto Santo">Porto Santo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alessandrini2012_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alessandrini2012-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1479 or 1480, Columbus's son <a href="/wiki/Diego_Columbus" title="Diego Columbus">Diego</a> was born. Between 1482 and 1485, Columbus traded along the coasts of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, reaching the Portuguese trading post of <a href="/wiki/Elmina" title="Elmina">Elmina</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_coast" title="Guinea coast">Guinea coast</a> (in present-day <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suranyi2015_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suranyi2015-38">[36]</a></sup> Before 1484, Columbus returned to Porto Santo to find that his wife had died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson199163_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson199163-39">[37]</a></sup> He returned to Portugal to settle her estate and take his son Diego with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taviani2016_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taviani2016-40">[38]</a></sup> He left Portugal for <a href="/wiki/Castile_(historical_region)" title="Castile (historical region)">Castile</a> in 1485, where he found a mistress in 1487, a 20-year-old orphan named <a href="/wiki/Beatriz_Enr%C3%ADquez_de_Arana" title="Beatriz Enríquez de Arana">Beatriz Enríquez de Arana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992126_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992126-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is likely that Beatriz met Columbus when he was in <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a>, a gathering site of many Genoese merchants and where the court of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a> was located at intervals. Beatriz, unmarried at the time, gave birth to Columbus's second son, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fernando_Columbus" title="Fernando Columbus">Fernando Columbus</a>, in July 1488, named for the monarch of Aragon. Columbus recognized the boy as his offspring. Columbus entrusted his older, legitimate son Diego to take care of Beatriz and pay the pension set aside for her following his death, but Diego was negligent in his duties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="968" data-file-width="1334" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/240px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/360px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/480px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Columbus's copy of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a></i>, with his handwritten notes in Latin written on the margins</div></div></div>
<p>Being ambitious, Columbus eventually learned <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, and Castilian. He read widely about astronomy, geography, and history, including the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" title="Claudius Ptolemy">Claudius Ptolemy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pierre_Cardinal_d%27Ailly" title="Pierre Cardinal d'Ailly">Pierre Cardinal d'Ailly</a>'s <i>Imago Mundi</i>, the travels of <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_John_Mandeville" title="Sir John Mandeville">Sir John Mandeville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_II" title="Pope Pius II">Pope Pius II</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Rerum_Ubique_Gestarum" title="Historia Rerum Ubique Gestarum">Historia Rerum Ubique Gestarum</a></i>. According to historian <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Morgan_(historian)" title="Edmund Morgan (historian)">Edmund Morgan</a>,
</p>
<blockquote><p>Columbus was not a scholarly man. Yet he studied these books, made hundreds of marginal notations in them and came out with ideas about the world that were characteristically simple and strong and sometimes wrong ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESMorgan_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESMorgan-42">[40]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quest_for_Asia">Quest for Asia</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Ocean,_Toscanelli,_1474.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="1990" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg/300px-Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg/450px-Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg/600px-Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Ocean,_Toscanelli,_1474.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paolo_dal_Pozzo_Toscanelli" title="Paolo dal Pozzo Toscanelli">Toscanelli</a>'s notions of the geography of the Atlantic Ocean (shown superimposed on a modern map), which directly influenced Columbus's plans</div></div></div>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>'s hegemony over <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a></i>, Europeans had long enjoyed a safe land passage on the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> to parts of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> (including <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a>, which were sources of valuable goods. With the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1453, the Silk Road was closed to Christian traders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DavidannGilbert2019_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-DavidannGilbert2019-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1474, the Florentine astronomer <a href="/wiki/Paolo_dal_Pozzo_Toscanelli" title="Paolo dal Pozzo Toscanelli">Paolo dal Pozzo Toscanelli</a> suggested to King <a href="/wiki/Afonso_V_of_Portugal" title="Afonso V of Portugal">Afonso V of Portugal</a> that sailing west across the Atlantic would be a quicker way to reach the <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Maluku</a> (Spice) Islands, China, and Japan than the route around Africa, but Afonso rejected his proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992108_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992108-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boxer1967_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boxer1967-45">[43]</a></sup> In the 1480s, Columbus and his brother proposed a plan to reach the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a> by sailing west. Columbus supposedly wrote Toscanelli in 1481 and received encouragement, along with a copy of a map the astronomer had sent Afonso implying that a westward route to Asia was possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992227_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992227-46">[44]</a></sup> Columbus's plans were complicated by the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Route" title="Cape Route">Cape Route</a> to Asia around Africa in 1488.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye2013_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye2013-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Carol_Delaney" title="Carol Delaney">Carol Delaney</a> and other commentators have argued that Columbus was a <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">Christian millennialist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apocalypticist" title="Apocalypticist">apocalypticist</a> and that these beliefs motivated his quest for Asia in a variety of ways. Columbus often wrote about seeking gold in the log books of his voyages and writes about acquiring the precious metal "in such quantity that the sovereigns... will undertake and prepare to go conquer the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Sepulcher" title="Holy Sepulcher">Holy Sepulcher</a>" in a fulfillment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_prophecy" title="Biblical prophecy">Biblical prophecy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[c]</a></sup> Columbus also often wrote about <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">converting</a> all races to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jstor3879352_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-jstor3879352-50">[47]</a></sup> Abbas Hamandi argues that Columbus was motivated by the hope of "[delivering] Jerusalem from Muslim hands"  by "using the resources of newly discovered lands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_considerations">Geographical considerations</span></h3>
<p>Despite <a href="/wiki/Myth_of_the_flat_Earth" title="Myth of the flat Earth">a popular misconception</a> to the contrary, nearly all educated Westerners of Columbus's time knew that the <a href="/wiki/Spherical_Earth" title="Spherical Earth">Earth is spherical</a>, a concept that had been understood since <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye2013244_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye2013244-52">[49]</a></sup> The techniques of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigation</a>, which uses the position of the Sun and the stars in the sky, had long been in use by astronomers and were beginning to be implemented by mariners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willoz-Egnor2013_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willoz-Egnor2013-53">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2002_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2002-54">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>As far back as the 3rd century BC, <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> had correctly computed the circumference of the Earth by using simple geometry and studying the shadows cast by objects at two remote locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> In the 1st century BC, <a href="/wiki/Posidonius" title="Posidonius">Posidonius</a> confirmed Eratosthenes's results by comparing stellar observations at two separate locations. These measurements were widely known among scholars, but Ptolemy's use of the smaller, old-fashioned units of distance led Columbus to underestimate the size of the Earth by about a third.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ColombusMap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8421" data-file-width="13616" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ColombusMap.jpg/350px-ColombusMap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ColombusMap.jpg/525px-ColombusMap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ColombusMap.jpg/700px-ColombusMap.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ColombusMap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Columbus map", drawn c. 1490 in the <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> mapmaking workshop of Bartolomeo and Christopher Columbus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Three <a href="/wiki/Cosmography" title="Cosmography">cosmographical</a> parameters determined the bounds of Columbus's enterprise: the distance across the ocean between Europe and Asia, which depended on the extent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oikumene" title="Oikumene">oikumene</a>, i.e., the Eurasian land-mass stretching east-west between Spain and China; the circumference of the Earth; and the number of miles or <a href="/wiki/League_(unit)" title="League (unit)">leagues</a> in a degree of <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, which was possible to deduce from the theory of the relationship between the size of the surfaces of water and the land as held by the followers of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> in medieval times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randles1990_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Randles1990-59">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>From <a href="/wiki/Pierre_d%27Ailly" title="Pierre d'Ailly">Pierre d'Ailly</a>'s <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Imago_Mundi_(Pierre_d%27Ailly)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Imago Mundi (Pierre d'Ailly) (page does not exist)">Imago Mundi</a></i> (1410), Columbus learned of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Muhammad_ibn_Kath%C4%ABr_al-Fargh%C4%81n%C4%AB" title="Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Kathīr al-Farghānī">Alfraganus</a>'s estimate that a degree of <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> (equal to approximately a degree of <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> along the equator) spanned 56.67 <a href="/wiki/Arabic_mile" title="Arabic mile">Arabic miles</a> (equivalent to 66.2 nautical miles, 122.6 kilometers or 76.2 mi), but he did not realize that this was expressed in the Arabic mile (about 1,830 meters or 1.14 mi) rather than the shorter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_mile" title="Roman mile">Roman mile</a> (about 1,480 m) with which he was familiar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahmud2017_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahmud2017-60">[57]</a></sup> Columbus therefore estimated the size of the Earth to be about 75% of Eratosthenes's calculation, and the distance westward from the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> to the Indies as only 68 degrees, equivalent to 3,080 nmi (5,700 km; 3,540 mi) (a 58% margin of error).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCormick2012_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCormick2012-61">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most scholars of the time accepted Ptolemy's estimate that <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> spanned 180° longitude,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gunn2018_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gunn2018-62">[59]</a></sup> rather than the actual 130° (to the Chinese mainland) or 150° (to Japan at the latitude of Spain). Columbus believed an even higher estimate, leaving a smaller percentage for water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zacher2016_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zacher2016-63">[60]</a></sup> In d'Ailly's <i>Imago Mundi</i>, Columbus read <a href="/wiki/Marinus_of_Tyre" title="Marinus of Tyre">Marinus of Tyre</a>'s estimate that the longitudinal span of Eurasia was 225° at the latitude of <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dilke2016_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dilke2016-64">[61]</a></sup> Some historians, such as Samuel Morison, have suggested that he followed the statement in the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_apocrypha" title="Biblical apocrypha">apocryphal</a> book <a href="/wiki/2_Esdras" title="2 Esdras">2 Esdras</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/II_Esdras#Chapter_6" title="wikisource:Bible (King James)/II Esdras">6:42</a>) that "six parts [of the globe] are habitable and the seventh is covered with water."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morison1974_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morison1974-65">[62]</a></sup> He was also aware of Marco Polo's claim that Japan (which he called "Cipangu") was some 2,414 km (1,500 mi) to the east of China ("Cathay"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butel2002_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butel2002-66">[63]</a></sup> and closer to the equator than it is. He was influenced by Toscanelli's idea that there were inhabited islands even farther to the east than Japan, including the mythical <a href="/wiki/Antillia" title="Antillia">Antillia</a>, which he thought might lie not much farther to the west than the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991-67">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on his sources, Columbus estimated a distance of 2,400 nmi (4,400 km; 2,800 mi) from the Canary Islands west to Japan; the actual distance is 10,600 nmi (19,600 km; 12,200 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992110_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992110-68">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edson2007_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edson2007-69">[66]</a></sup> No ship in the 15th century could have carried enough food and fresh water for such a long voyage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2002_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2002-70">[67]</a></sup> and the dangers involved in navigating through the uncharted ocean would have been formidable. Most European navigators reasonably concluded that a westward voyage from Europe to Asia was unfeasible. The Catholic Monarchs, however, having completed the <i><a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a></i>, an expensive war against the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, were eager to obtain a competitive edge over other European countries in the quest for trade with the Indies. Columbus's project, though far-fetched, held the promise of such an advantage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nautical_considerations">Nautical considerations</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="#Navigational_expertise">§ Navigational expertise</a></div>
<p>Though Columbus was wrong about the number of degrees of longitude that separated Europe from the Far East and about the distance that each degree represented, he did take advantage of the <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">trade winds</a>, which would prove to be the key to his successful navigation of the Atlantic Ocean. He planned to first sail to the Canary Islands before continuing west with the northeast trade wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gómez2008_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gómez2008-72">[69]</a></sup> Part of the return to Spain would require traveling against the wind using an arduous sailing technique called <a href="/wiki/Point_of_sail#Close-hauled" title="Point of sail">beating</a>, during which progress is made very slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991132_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991132-73">[70]</a></sup> To effectively make the return voyage, Columbus would need to follow the curving trade winds northeastward to the middle latitudes of the North Atlantic, where he would be able to catch the "<a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">westerlies</a>" that blow eastward to the coast of Western Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991314_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991314-74">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The navigational technique for travel in the Atlantic appears to have been exploited first by the Portuguese, who referred to it as the <i><a href="/wiki/Volta_do_mar" title="Volta do mar">volta do mar</a></i> ('turn of the sea'). Through his marriage to his first wife, Felipa Perestrello, Columbus had access to the nautical charts and logs that had belonged to her deceased father, Bartolomeu Perestrello, who had served as a captain in the Portuguese navy under <a href="/wiki/Prince_Henry_the_Navigator" title="Prince Henry the Navigator">Prince Henry the Navigator</a>. In the mapmaking shop where he worked with his brother Bartolomeo, Columbus also had ample opportunity to hear the stories of old seamen about their voyages to the western seas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rickey1992_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rickey1992-75">[72]</a></sup> but his knowledge of the Atlantic wind patterns was still imperfect at the time of his first voyage. By sailing due west from the Canary Islands during <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="Atlantic hurricane season">hurricane season</a>, skirting the so-called <a href="/wiki/Horse_latitudes" title="Horse latitudes">horse latitudes</a> of the mid-Atlantic, he risked being becalmed and running into a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclone</a>, both of which he avoided by chance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991198–99_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991198–99-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quest_for_financial_support_for_a_voyage">Quest for financial support for a voyage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_(cropped).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="415" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png/170px-Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png/255px-Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png/340px-Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_(cropped).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Columbus offers his services to the King of Portugal</i>; <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Chodowiecki" title="Daniel Chodowiecki">Chodowiecki</a>, 17th century</div></div></div>
<p>By about 1484, Columbus proposed his planned voyage to King <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">John II of Portugal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rickey1992224_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rickey1992224-77">[74]</a></sup> The king submitted Columbus's proposal to his advisors, who rejected it, correctly, on the grounds that Columbus's estimate for a voyage of 2,400 nmi was only a quarter of what it should have been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison199168–70_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison199168–70-78">[75]</a></sup> In 1488, Columbus again appealed to the court of Portugal, and John II again granted him an audience. That meeting also proved unsuccessful, in part because not long afterwards <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a> returned to Portugal with news of his successful rounding of the southern tip of Africa (near the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinheiro-Marques2016_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinheiro-Marques2016-79">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SymcoxSullivan2016_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-SymcoxSullivan2016-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Columbus sought an audience with the monarchs <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand II of Aragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a>, who had united several kingdoms in the Iberian Peninsula by marrying and were now ruling together. On 1 May 1486, permission having been granted, Columbus presented his plans to Queen Isabella, who, in turn, referred it to a committee. The learned men of Spain, like their counterparts in Portugal, replied that Columbus had grossly underestimated the distance to Asia. They pronounced the idea impractical and advised the Catholic Monarchs to pass on the proposed venture. To keep Columbus from taking his ideas elsewhere, and perhaps to keep their options open, the sovereigns gave him an allowance, totaling about 14,000 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maravedis" title="Maravedis">maravedis</a></i> for the year, or about the annual salary of a sailor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson199184_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson199184-81">[78]</a></sup> In May 1489, the queen sent him another 10,000 <i>maravedis</i>, and the same year the monarchs furnished him with a letter ordering all cities and towns under their dominion to provide him food and lodging at no cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Columbus also dispatched his brother <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Columbus" title="Bartholomew Columbus">Bartolomeo</a> to the court of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII of England</a> to inquire whether the English crown might sponsor his expedition, but he was captured by pirates en route, and only arrived in early 1491.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson199186,_92_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson199186,_92-83">[80]</a></sup> By that time, Columbus had retreated to <a href="/wiki/La_R%C3%A1bida_Friary" title="La Rábida Friary">La Rábida Friary</a>, where the Spanish crown sent him 20,000 <i>maravedis</i> to buy new clothes and instructions to return to the <a href="/wiki/Cortes_Generales" title="Cortes Generales">Spanish court</a> for renewed discussions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson199192_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson199192-84">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agreement_with_the_Spanish_crown">Agreement with the Spanish crown</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Various_roofs_and_towers_of_Alhambra,_from_Generalife_gardens,_Granada,_Spain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2809" data-file-width="4489" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Various_roofs_and_towers_of_Alhambra%2C_from_Generalife_gardens%2C_Granada%2C_Spain.jpg/220px-Various_roofs_and_towers_of_Alhambra%2C_from_Generalife_gardens%2C_Granada%2C_Spain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Various_roofs_and_towers_of_Alhambra%2C_from_Generalife_gardens%2C_Granada%2C_Spain.jpg/330px-Various_roofs_and_towers_of_Alhambra%2C_from_Generalife_gardens%2C_Granada%2C_Spain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Various_roofs_and_towers_of_Alhambra%2C_from_Generalife_gardens%2C_Granada%2C_Spain.jpg/440px-Various_roofs_and_towers_of_Alhambra%2C_from_Generalife_gardens%2C_Granada%2C_Spain.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Various_roofs_and_towers_of_Alhambra,_from_Generalife_gardens,_Granada,_Spain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a>, where Columbus received permission from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a> for his first voyage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Columbus waited at King Ferdinand's camp until Ferdinand and Isabella conquered <a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">Granada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">last Muslim stronghold</a> on the Iberian Peninsula, in January 1492. A council led by Isabella's confessor, <a href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Talavera" title="Hernando de Talavera">Hernando de Talavera</a>, found Columbus's proposal to reach the Indies implausible. Columbus had left for France when Ferdinand intervened,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[d]</a></sup> first sending Talavera and Bishop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diego_Deza" title="Diego Deza">Diego Deza</a> to appeal to the queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992131–32_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992131–32-86">[83]</a></sup> Isabella was finally convinced by the king's clerk <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Sant%C3%A1ngel" title="Luis de Santángel">Luis de Santángel</a>, who argued that Columbus would take his ideas elsewhere, and offered to help arrange the funding. Isabella then sent a royal guard to fetch Columbus, who had traveled 2 leagues (over 10 km) toward Córdoba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992131–32_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992131–32-86">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the April 1492 "<a href="/wiki/Capitulations_of_Santa_Fe" title="Capitulations of Santa Fe">Capitulations of Santa Fe</a>", King Ferdinand and Queen Isabella promised Columbus that if he succeeded he would be given the rank of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Admiral_of_the_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Admiral of the Ocean (page does not exist)">Admiral of the Ocean</a> Sea and appointed <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">Viceroy</a> and Governor of all the new lands he might claim for Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lantigua2020_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lantigua2020-88">[84]</a></sup> He had the right to nominate three persons, from whom the sovereigns would choose one, for any office in the new lands. He would be entitled to 10% (<i><a href="/wiki/Tithe" title="Tithe">diezmo</a></i>) of all the revenues from the new lands in perpetuity. He also would have the option of buying one-eighth interest in any commercial venture in the new lands, and receive one-eighth (<i>ochavo</i>) of the profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991662_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991662-89">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-González-Sánchez2006_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-González-Sánchez2006-90">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1500, during his third voyage to the Americas, Columbus was arrested and dismissed from his posts. He and his sons, Diego and Fernando, then conducted a lengthy series of court cases against the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castilian crown</a>, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Pleitos_colombinos" title="Pleitos colombinos">pleitos colombinos</a></i>, alleging that the Crown had illegally reneged on its contractual obligations to Columbus and his heirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Márquez1982_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Márquez1982-92">[88]</a></sup> The Columbus family had some success in their first litigation, as a judgment of 1511 confirmed Diego's position as viceroy but reduced his powers. Diego resumed litigation in 1512, which lasted until 1536, and further disputes initiated by heirs continued until 1790.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald2005_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald2005-93">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Voyages">Voyages</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">Voyages of Christopher Columbus</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="#Legacy">§ Legacy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Christopher_Columbus.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Flag_of_Christopher_Columbus.svg/240px-Flag_of_Christopher_Columbus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Flag_of_Christopher_Columbus.svg/360px-Flag_of_Christopher_Columbus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Flag_of_Christopher_Columbus.svg/480px-Flag_of_Christopher_Columbus.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Christopher_Columbus.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Captain's ensign of Columbus's ships</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viajes_de_colon_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/350px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/525px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/700px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viajes_de_colon_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The voyages of Christopher Columbus (conjectural)</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1492 and 1504, Columbus completed four round-trip voyages between Spain and the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, each voyage being sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Crown of Castile</a>. On his first voyage he reached the Americas, initiating the European <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_the_Americas" title="Exploration of the Americas">exploration</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">colonization of the continent</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a>. His role in history is thus important to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Western_civilization" title="History of Western civilization">Western history</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">human history</a> writ large.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpechtStockland2017_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpechtStockland2017-94">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Columbus%27s_letter_on_the_first_voyage" title="Columbus's letter on the first voyage">Columbus's letter on the first voyage</a>, published following his first return to Spain, he claimed that he had reached Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991381_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991381-95">[91]</a></sup> as previously described by Marco Polo and other Europeans. Over his subsequent voyages, Columbus refused to acknowledge that the lands he visited and claimed for Spain were not part of Asia, in the face of mounting evidence to the contrary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horodowich2017_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horodowich2017-96">[92]</a></sup> This might explain, in part, why the American continent was named after the <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florentine</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Amerigo Vespucci">Amerigo Vespucci</a>—who received credit for recognizing it as a "<a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>"—and not after Columbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umc_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-umc-97">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-incognita_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-incognita-99">[e]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="First_voyage_.281492.E2.80.931493.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_voyage_(1492–1493)">First voyage (1492–1493)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_first_voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="1001" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Columbus_first_voyage.jpg/350px-Columbus_first_voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Columbus_first_voyage.jpg/525px-Columbus_first_voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Columbus_first_voyage.jpg/700px-Columbus_first_voyage.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_first_voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First voyage (conjectural).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstimage_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-firstimage-102">[f]</a></sup> Modern place names in black, Columbus's place names in blue</div></div></div>
<p>On the evening of 3 August 1492, Columbus departed from <a href="/wiki/Palos_de_la_Frontera" title="Palos de la Frontera">Palos de la Frontera</a> with three ships. The largest was a <a href="/wiki/Carrack" title="Carrack">carrack</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_(ship)" title="Santa María (ship)">Santa María</a></i>, owned and captained by <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_la_Cosa" title="Juan de la Cosa">Juan de la Cosa</a>, and under Columbus's direct command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991102_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991102-103">[97]</a></sup> The other two were smaller <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravels</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Pinta_(ship)" title="Pinta (ship)">Pinta</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Ni%C3%B1a" title="Niña">Niña</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup> piloted by the <a href="/wiki/Pinz%C3%B3n_brothers" title="Pinzón brothers">Pinzón brothers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991102_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991102-103">[97]</a></sup> Columbus first sailed to the Canary Islands. There he restocked provisions and made repairs then departed from <a href="/wiki/San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_de_La_Gomera" title="San Sebastián de La Gomera">San Sebastián de La Gomera</a> on 6 September,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992146–47_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992146–47-105">[99]</a></sup> for what turned out to be a five-week voyage across the ocean.
</p><p>
On 7 October, the crew spotted "[i]mmense flocks of birds".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> On 11 October, Columbus changed the fleet's course to due west, and sailed through the night, believing land was soon to be found. At around 02:00 the following morning, a lookout on the <i>Pinta</i>, <a href="/wiki/Rodrigo_de_Triana" title="Rodrigo de Triana">Rodrigo de Triana</a>, spotted land. The captain of the <i>Pinta</i>, <a href="/wiki/Mart%C3%ADn_Alonso_Pinz%C3%B3n" title="Martín Alonso Pinzón">Martín Alonso Pinzón</a>, verified the sight of land and alerted Columbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991226_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991226-107">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup> Columbus later maintained that he had already seen a light on the land a few hours earlier, thereby claiming for himself the lifetime pension promised by Ferdinand and Isabella to the first person to sight land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye2013_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye2013-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> Columbus called this island (in what is now the Bahamas) <i>San Salvador</i> (meaning "Holy Savior"); <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">the natives</a> called it <a href="/wiki/Guanahani" title="Guanahani">Guanahani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen201199_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen201199-110">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[g]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus%27s_journal" title="Christopher Columbus's journal">Christopher Columbus's journal</a> entry of 12 October 1492 states:</p><blockquote><p>I saw some who had marks of wounds on their bodies and I made signs to them asking what they were; and they showed me how people from other islands nearby came there and tried to take them, and how they defended themselves; and I believed and believe that they come here from <i>tierra firme</i> to take them captive. They should be good and intelligent servants, for I see that they say very quickly everything that is said to them; and I believe they would become Christians very easily, for it seemed to me that they had no religion. Our Lord pleasing, at the time of my departure I will take six of them from here to Your Highnesses in order that they may learn to speak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DunnKelly1989_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-DunnKelly1989-113">[106]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Columbus called the inhabitants of the lands that he visited <i>Los Indios</i> (Spanish for "Indians").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoxie_1996_p._114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoxie_1996_p.-114">[107]</a></sup> He initially encountered the <a href="/wiki/Lucayan_people" title="Lucayan people">Lucayan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno" title="Taíno">Taíno</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arawak" title="Arawak">Arawak</a> peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keegan2015_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keegan2015-115">[108]</a></sup> Noting their gold ear ornaments, Columbus took some of the Arawaks prisoner and insisted that they guide him to the source of the gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-116">[109]</a></sup> Columbus did not believe he needed to create a fortified outpost, writing, "the people here are simple in war-like matters ... I could conquer the whole of them with fifty men, and govern them as I pleased."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[110]</a></sup> The Taínos told Columbus that another indigenous tribe, <a href="/wiki/Kalinago" title="Kalinago">Caribs</a>, were fierce warriors and <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">cannibals</a>, who made frequent raids on the Taínos, often capturing their women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Columbus also explored the northeast coast of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, where he landed on 28 October. On the night of 26 November, Martín Alonso Pinzón took the <i>Pinta</i> on an unauthorized expedition in search of an island called "Babeque" or "Baneque",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter2012_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter2012-120">[113]</a></sup> which the natives had told him was rich in gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magasich-AirolaBeer2007_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magasich-AirolaBeer2007-121">[114]</a></sup> Columbus, for his part, continued to the northern coast of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>, where he landed on 6 December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson-Córdova2017_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-Córdova2017-122">[115]</a></sup> There, the <i>Santa María</i> ran aground on 25 December 1492 and had to be abandoned. The wreck was used as a target for cannon fire to impress the native peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye201331–32_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye201331–32-123">[116]</a></sup> Columbus was received by the native <i><a href="/wiki/Cacique" title="Cacique">cacique</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guacanagari" title="Guacanagari">Guacanagari</a>, who gave him permission to leave some of his men behind. Columbus left 39 men, including the interpreter <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Torres" title="Luis de Torres">Luis de Torres</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991145_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991145-124">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[h]</a></sup> and founded the settlement of <a href="/wiki/La_Navidad" title="La Navidad">La Navidad</a>, in present-day <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeaganCruxent1993_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeaganCruxent1993-126">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maclean2008_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maclean2008-127">[119]</a></sup> Columbus took more natives prisoner and continued his exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-116">[109]</a></sup> He kept sailing along the northern coast of Hispaniola with a single ship until he encountered Pinzón and the <i>Pinta</i> on 6 January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gužauskytė2014_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gužauskytė2014-128">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 13 January 1493, Columbus made his last stop of this voyage in the Americas, in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Rinc%C3%B3n" title="Bay of Rincón">Bay of Rincón</a> in northeast Hispaniola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> There he encountered the <a href="/wiki/Ciguayos" title="Ciguayos">Ciguayos</a>, the only natives who offered violent resistance during this voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup> The Ciguayos refused to trade the amount of bows and arrows that Columbus desired; in the ensuing clash one Ciguayo was stabbed in the buttocks and another wounded with an arrow in his chest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[123]</a></sup> Because of these events, Columbus called the inlet the <i>Golfo de Las Flechas</i> (<a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Arrows" title="Bay of Arrows">Bay of Arrows</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DunnKelly1989341_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-DunnKelly1989341-132">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Columbus headed for Spain on the <i>Niña</i>, but a storm separated him from the <i>Pinta,</i> and forced the <i>Niña</i> to stop at the island of Santa Maria in the Azores. Half of his crew went ashore to say prayers of thanksgiving in a chapel for having survived the storm. But while praying, they were imprisoned by the governor of the island, ostensibly on suspicion of being pirates.  After a two-day standoff, the prisoners were released, and Columbus again set sail for Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catz1990_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Catz1990-133">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another storm forced Columbus into the port at <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye2013_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurphyCoye2013-47">[45]</a></sup> From there he went to <i>Vale do Paraíso</i> north of Lisbon to meet King John II of Portugal, who told Columbus that he believed the voyage to be in violation of the 1479 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alc%C3%A1%C3%A7ovas" title="Treaty of Alcáçovas">Treaty of Alcáçovas</a>. After spending more than a week in Portugal, Columbus set sail for Spain. Returning to Palos on 15 March 1493, he was given a hero's welcome and soon afterward received by Isabella and Ferdinand in Barcelona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kamen2014_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kamen2014-134">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Columbus's letter on the first voyage, dispatched to the Spanish court, was instrumental in spreading the news throughout Europe about his voyage. Almost immediately after his arrival in Spain, printed versions began to appear, and word of his voyage spread rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ife1992_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ife1992-135">[127]</a></sup> Most people initially believed that he had reached Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991381_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991381-95">[91]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bulls_of_Donation" title="Bulls of Donation">Bulls of Donation</a>, three papal bulls of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a> delivered in 1493, purported to grant overseas territories to <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and the Catholic Monarchs of Spain. They were replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">Treaty of Tordesillas</a> of 1494.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>The two earliest published copies of Columbus's letter on the first voyage aboard the <a href="/wiki/Ni%C3%B1a" title="Niña">Niña</a> were donated in 2017 by the Jay I. Kislak Foundation to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Miami" title="University of Miami">University of Miami</a> library in <a href="/wiki/Coral_Gables,_Florida" title="Coral Gables, Florida">Coral Gables, Florida</a>, where they are housed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Second_voyage_.281493.E2.80.931496.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_voyage_(1493–1496)">Second voyage (1493–1496)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_second_voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="563" data-file-width="1001" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Columbus_second_voyage.jpg/350px-Columbus_second_voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Columbus_second_voyage.jpg/525px-Columbus_second_voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Columbus_second_voyage.jpg/700px-Columbus_second_voyage.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_second_voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Columbus's second voyage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[i]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>On 24 September 1493, Columbus sailed from <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">Cádiz</a> with 17 ships, and supplies to establish permanent colonies in the Americas. He sailed with nearly 1,500 men, including sailors, soldiers, priests, carpenters, stonemasons, metalworkers, and farmers. Among the expedition members were <a href="/wiki/Diego_%C3%81lvarez_Chanca" title="Diego Álvarez Chanca">Alvarez Chanca</a>, a physician who wrote a detailed account of the second voyage; Juan Ponce de León, the first governor of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> and Florida; the father of Bartolomé de las Casas; <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_la_Cosa" title="Juan de la Cosa">Juan de la Cosa</a>, a cartographer who is credited with making the first <a href="/wiki/Map_of_Juan_de_la_Cosa" title="Map of Juan de la Cosa">world map depicting the New World</a>; and Columbus's youngest brother Diego.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeaganCruxent2008_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeaganCruxent2008-140">[131]</a></sup> The fleet stopped at the Canary Islands to take on more supplies, and set sail again on 7 October, deliberately taking a more southerly course than on the first voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedini2016705_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bedini2016705-141">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 3 November, they arrived in the <a href="/wiki/Windward_Islands" title="Windward Islands">Windward Islands</a>; the first island they encountered was named <a href="/wiki/Dominica" title="Dominica">Dominica</a> by Columbus, but not finding a good harbor there, they anchored off a nearby smaller island, which he named <i>Mariagalante</i>, now a part of <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">Guadeloupe</a> and called <a href="/wiki/Marie-Galante" title="Marie-Galante">Marie-Galante</a>. Other islands named by Columbus on this voyage were <a href="/wiki/Montserrat" title="Montserrat">Montserrat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antigua" title="Antigua">Antigua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Martin_(island)" title="Saint Martin (island)">Saint Martin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Islands" title="Virgin Islands">Virgin Islands</a>, as well as many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedini2016705_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bedini2016705-141">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 22 November, Columbus returned to Hispaniola to visit La Navidad, where 39 Spaniards had been left during the first voyage. Columbus found the fort in ruins, destroyed by the Taínos after some of the Spaniards reportedly antagonized their hosts with their unrestrained lust for gold and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991423–27_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991423–27-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeaganCruxent1993_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeaganCruxent1993-126">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[134]</a></sup> Columbus then established a poorly located and short-lived settlement to the east, <a href="/wiki/La_Isabela" title="La Isabela">La Isabela</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeaganCruxent2008_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeaganCruxent2008-140">[131]</a></sup> in the present-day <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>From April to August 1494, Columbus explored Cuba and Jamaica, then returned to Hispaniola. By the end of 1494, disease and famine had killed two-thirds of the Spanish settlers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Austin-Alchon2003_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-Alchon2003-145">[136]</a></sup> Columbus implemented <i><a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">encomienda</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[138]</a></sup> a Spanish labor system that rewarded conquerors with the labor of conquered non-Christian people.
Columbus executed Spanish colonists for minor crimes, and used <a href="/wiki/Dismemberment" title="Dismemberment">dismemberment</a> as punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-148">[139]</a></sup> Columbus and the colonists enslaved the indigenous people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991482–85_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991482–85-149">[140]</a></sup> including children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[141]</a></sup> Natives were beaten, raped, and tortured for the location of imagined gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stannard1993_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stannard1993-151">[142]</a></sup> Thousands committed suicide rather than face the oppression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[j]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1495, Columbus rounded up about 1,500 Arawaks, some of whom had rebelled, in a great slave raid. About 500 of the strongest were shipped to Spain as slaves,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991183,_190_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991183,_190-155">[145]</a></sup> with about two hundred of those dying en route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-116">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenPenman2017_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-CohenPenman2017-156">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 1495, the Spanish crown sent ships and supplies to Hispaniola. In October, Florentine merchant Gianotto Berardi, who had won the contract to provision the fleet of Columbus's second voyage and to supply the colony on Hispaniola, received almost 40,000 <i><a href="/wiki/Maraved%C3%AD" title="Maravedí">maravedís</a></i> worth of enslaved Indians. He renewed his effort to get supplies to Columbus, and was working to organize a fleet when he suddenly died in December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Felipe2007_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Felipe2007-157">[147]</a></sup> On 10 March 1496, having been away about 30 months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991497_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991497-158">[148]</a></sup> the fleet departed La Isabela. On 8 June the crew sighted land somewhere between Lisbon and <a href="/wiki/Cape_St._Vincent" title="Cape St. Vincent">Cape St. Vincent</a>, and disembarked in Cádiz on 11 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook_1998_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook_1998-159">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Third_voyage_.281498.E2.80.931500.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_voyage_(1498–1500)">Third voyage (1498–1500)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_third_voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="638" data-file-width="901" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Columbus_third_voyage.jpg/350px-Columbus_third_voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Columbus_third_voyage.jpg/525px-Columbus_third_voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Columbus_third_voyage.jpg/700px-Columbus_third_voyage.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_third_voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Third voyage</div></div></div>
<p>On 30 May 1498, Columbus left with six ships from <a href="/wiki/Sanl%C3%BAcar_de_Barrameda" title="Sanlúcar de Barrameda">Sanlúcar, Spain</a>. The fleet called at Madeira and the Canary Islands, where it divided in two, with three ships heading for Hispaniola and the other three vessels, commanded by Columbus, sailing south to the Cape Verde Islands and then westward across the Atlantic. It is probable that this expedition was intended at least partly to confirm rumors of a large continent south of the Caribbean Sea, that is, South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saunders2005_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders2005-160">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 31 July they sighted <a href="/wiki/Trinidad" title="Trinidad">Trinidad</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flint2017_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flint2017-161">[151]</a></sup> the most southerly of the Caribbean islands.
