On 5 August, Columbus sent several small boats ashore on the southern side of the <a href="/wiki/Paria_Peninsula" title="Paria Peninsula">Paria Peninsula</a> in what is now Venezuela,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1997_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1997-162">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011249_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011249-163">[153]</a></sup> near the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Orinoco" title="Orinoco">Orinoco</a> river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saunders2005_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders2005-160">[150]</a></sup>  This was the first recorded landing of Europeans on the mainland of South America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1997_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1997-162">[152]</a></sup> which Columbus realized must be a continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeruvabel2003_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeruvabel2003-164">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cervantes2021_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cervantes2021-165">[155]</a></sup> The fleet then sailed to the islands of <a href="/wiki/Chacachacare" title="Chacachacare">Chacachacare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margarita_Island" title="Margarita Island">Margarita</a>, reaching the latter on 14 August,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011258_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011258-166">[156]</a></sup> and sighted <a href="/wiki/Tobago" title="Tobago">Tobago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">Grenada</a> from afar, according to some scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorisonObregón1964_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-MorisonObregón1964-167">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1997_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1997-162">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 19 August, Columbus returned to Hispaniola. There he found settlers in rebellion against his rule, and his unfulfilled promises of riches. Columbus had some of the Europeans tried for their disobedience; at least one rebel leader was hanged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011284–85_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011284–85-168">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 1499, Columbus sent two ships to Spain, asking the Court of Spain to appoint a royal commissioner to help him govern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[159]</a></sup> By this time, accusations of tyranny and incompetence on the part of Columbus had also reached the Court. The sovereigns sent <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Bobadilla" title="Francisco de Bobadilla">Francisco de Bobadilla</a>, a relative of Marquesa <a href="/wiki/Beatriz_de_Bobadilla" title="Beatriz de Bobadilla">Beatriz de Bobadilla</a>, a patron of Columbus and a close friend of <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Queen Isabella</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofman1994_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hofman1994-170">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992125_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992125-171">[161]</a></sup> to investigate the accusations of brutality made against the Admiral. Arriving in Santo Domingo while Columbus was away, Bobadilla was immediately met with complaints about all three Columbus brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011276–77_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011276–77-172">[162]</a></sup> He moved into Columbus's house and seized his property, took depositions from the Admiral's enemies, and declared himself governor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1997_162-3"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1997-162">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bobadilla reported to Spain that Columbus once punished a man found guilty of stealing corn by having his ears and nose cut off and then selling him into slavery. He claimed that Columbus regularly used <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mutilation" title="Mutilation">mutilation</a> to govern Hispaniola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[k]</a></sup> Testimony recorded in the report stated that Columbus congratulated his brother Bartolomeo on "defending the family" when the latter ordered a woman paraded naked through the streets and then had her tongue cut because she had "spoken ill of the admiral and his brothers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011283_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011283-175">[164]</a></sup> The document also describes how Columbus put down native unrest and revolt: he first ordered a brutal suppression of the uprising in which many natives were killed, and then paraded their dismembered bodies through the streets in an attempt to discourage further rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A&amp;E_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-A&amp;E-176">[165]</a></sup> Columbus vehemently denied the charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-177">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[167]</a></sup> The neutrality and accuracy of the accusations and investigations of Bobadilla toward Columbus and his brothers have been disputed by historians, given the anti-Italian sentiment of the Spaniards and Bobadilla's desire to take over Columbus's position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nas.org_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-nas.org-180">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cervantes202146_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cervantes202146-181">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>In early October 1500, Columbus and Diego presented themselves to Bobadilla, and were put in chains aboard <i>La Gorda</i>, the caravel on which Bobadilla had arrived at Santo Domingo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011276_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011276-182">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gužauskytė2014179_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gužauskytė2014179-183">[172]</a></sup> They were returned to Spain, and languished in jail for six weeks before King Ferdinand ordered their release. Not long after, the king and queen summoned the Columbus brothers to the <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a> palace in <a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">Granada</a>. The sovereigns expressed indignation at Bobadilla's actions, who was then recalled and ordered to make restitutions of the property he had confiscated from Columbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-177">[166]</a></sup> The royal couple heard the brothers' pleas; restored their freedom and wealth; and, after much persuasion, agreed to fund Columbus's fourth voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[173]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_de_Ovando" title="Nicolás de Ovando">Nicolás de Ovando</a> was to replace Bobadilla and be the new governor of the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-185">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>New light was shed on the seizure of Columbus and his brother Bartolomeo, the <a href="/wiki/Adelantado" title="Adelantado">Adelantado</a>, with the discovery by archivist Isabel Aguirre of an incomplete copy of the testimonies against them gathered by Francisco de Bobadilla at Santo Domingo in 1500. She found a manuscript copy of this <i>pesquisa</i> (inquiry) ‌in the Archive of Simancas, Spain, uncatalogued until she and Consuelo Varela published their book, <i>La caída de Cristóbal Colón: el juicio de Bobadilla</i> (<i>The fall of Christopher Colón: the judgement of Bobadilla</i>) in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leon2012_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leon2012-186">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VarelaAguirre2006_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-VarelaAguirre2006-187">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Fourth_voyage_.281502.E2.80.931504.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth_voyage_(1502–1504)">Fourth voyage (1502–1504)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="1001" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg/350px-Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg/525px-Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg/700px-Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Columbus's fourth voyage</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:House_of_Colon_COA_(2).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg/200px-House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg/300px-House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg/400px-House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:House_of_Colon_COA_(2).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">Coat of arms</a> granted to Christopher Columbus and the <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Col%C3%B3n_(familia)" title="es:Colón (familia)">House of Colon</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Motu_proprio" title="Motu proprio">motu proprio</a></i> in 1502</div></div></div>
<p>On 9 May 1502,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[177]</a></sup> Columbus left Cádiz with his flagship <i>Santa María</i> and three other vessels. The ships were crewed by 140 men, including his brother Bartolomeo as second in command and his son Fernando.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sauer2008_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sauer2008-189">[178]</a></sup> He sailed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arzila" title="Arzila">Arzila</a> on the Moroccan coast to rescue Portuguese soldiers said to be besieged by the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>. The siege had been lifted by the time they arrived, so the Spaniards stayed only a day and continued on to the Canary Islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook199846_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook199846-190">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 15 June, the fleet arrived at <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a>, where it lingered for several days. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">hurricane</a> was forming, so Columbus continued westward,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sauer2008_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sauer2008-189">[178]</a></sup> hoping to find shelter on Hispaniola. He arrived at <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a> on 29 June, but was denied port, and the new governor Francisco de Bobadilla refused to listen to his warning that a hurricane was approaching. Instead, while Columbus's ships sheltered at the mouth of the Rio Jaina, the first <a href="/wiki/Spanish_treasure_fleet" title="Spanish treasure fleet">Spanish treasure fleet</a> sailed into the hurricane. Columbus's ships survived with only minor damage, while 20 of the 30 ships in the governor's fleet were lost along with 500 lives (including that of Francisco de Bobadilla). Although a few surviving ships managed to straggle back to Santo Domingo, <i>Aguja</i>, the fragile ship carrying Columbus's personal belongings and his 4,000 <i>pesos</i> in gold was the sole vessel to reach Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011288–89,_302–3_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011288–89,_302–3-191">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gužauskytė2014185_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gužauskytė2014185-192">[181]</a></sup> The gold was his <i>tenth</i> (<i>décimo</i>) of the profits from Hispaniola, equal to 240,000 maravedis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedini2016200_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bedini2016200-193">[182]</a></sup> guaranteed by the Catholic Monarchs in 1492.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armas1985_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armas1985-194">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>After a brief stop at <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, Columbus sailed to Central America, arriving at the coast of <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> on 30 July. Here Bartolomeo found native merchants and a large canoe. On 14 August, Columbus landed on the continental mainland at Punta Caxinas, now <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Castilla,_Honduras" title="Puerto Castilla, Honduras">Puerto Castilla, Honduras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colindres1975_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colindres1975-195">[184]</a></sup> He spent two months exploring the coasts of Honduras, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, seeking a strait in the western Caribbean through which he could sail to the Indian Ocean. Sailing south along the Nicaraguan coast, he found a channel that led into Almirante Bay in <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> on 5 October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calvo2004_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calvo2004-196">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedini2016720_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bedini2016720-197">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>As soon as his ships anchored in Almirante Bay, Columbus encountered <a href="/wiki/Ng%C3%A4be" title="Ngäbe">Ngäbe</a> people in canoes who were wearing gold ornaments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StirlingStirling1964_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-StirlingStirling1964-198">[187]</a></sup> In January 1503, he established a <a href="/wiki/Garrison" title="Garrison">garrison</a> at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Bel%C3%A9n_River" title="Belén River">Belén River</a>. Columbus left for Hispaniola on 16 April. On 10 May he sighted the <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a>, naming them "<i>Las Tortugas</i>" after the numerous <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a> there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011330_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011330-199">[188]</a></sup> His ships sustained damage in a storm off the coast of Cuba. Unable to travel farther, on 25 June 1503 they were beached in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Ann_Parish,_Jamaica" title="Saint Ann Parish, Jamaica">Saint Ann Parish, Jamaica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011330–332_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011330–332-200">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>For six months Columbus and 230 of his men remained stranded on Jamaica. Diego Méndez de Segura, who had shipped out as a personal secretary to Columbus, and a Spanish shipmate called Bartolomé Flisco, along with six natives, paddled a canoe to get help from Hispaniola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roorda2020_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roorda2020-201">[190]</a></sup> The governor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_de_Ovando_y_C%C3%A1ceres" title="Nicolás de Ovando y Cáceres">Nicolás de Ovando y Cáceres</a>, detested Columbus and obstructed all efforts to rescue him and his men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vigneras1978_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vigneras1978-202">[191]</a></sup> In the meantime Columbus, in a desperate effort to induce the natives to continue provisioning him and his hungry men, won their favor by predicting a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/February_1504_lunar_eclipse" title="February 1504 lunar eclipse">lunar eclipse for 29 February 1504</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Zacuto" title="Abraham Zacuto">Abraham Zacuto</a>'s astronomical charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hakin2002_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hakin2002-203">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kadir1992_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kadir1992-205">[194]</a></sup> Despite the governor's obstruction, Christopher Columbus and his men were rescued on 28 June 1504, and arrived in Sanlúcar, Spain, on 7 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vigneras1978_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vigneras1978-202">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Later_life.2C_illness.2C_and_death"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life,_illness,_and_death">Later life, illness, and death</span></h2>
<p>Columbus had always claimed that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conversion_(religion)" title="Conversion (religion)">conversion</a> of non-believers was one reason for his explorations, and he grew increasingly religious in his later years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RiveraPagán1992_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-RiveraPagán1992-206">[195]</a></sup> Probably with the assistance of his son Diego and his friend the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthusian" title="Carthusian">Carthusian</a> monk Gaspar Gorricio, Columbus produced two books during his later years: a <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Privileges" title="Book of Privileges">Book of Privileges</a></i> (1502), detailing and documenting the rewards from the Spanish Crown to which he believed he and his heirs were entitled, and a <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Prophecies" title="Book of Prophecies">Book of Prophecies</a></i> (1505), in which passages from the Bible were used to place his achievements as an explorer in the context of <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watts1985_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watts1985-207">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his later years, Columbus demanded that the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Crown of Castile</a> give him his tenth of all the riches and trade goods yielded by the new lands, as stipulated in the <a href="/wiki/Capitulations_of_Santa_Fe" title="Capitulations of Santa Fe">Capitulations of Santa Fe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-González-Sánchez2006_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-González-Sánchez2006-90">[86]</a></sup> Because he had been relieved of his duties as governor, the Crown did not feel bound by that contract and his demands were rejected. After his death, his heirs sued the Crown for a part of the profits from trade with America, as well as other rewards. This led to a protracted series of legal disputes known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Pleitos_colombinos" title="Pleitos colombinos">pleitos colombinos</a></i> ("Columbian lawsuits").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald2005_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald2005-93">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>During a violent storm on his first return voyage, Columbus, then 41, had suffered an attack of what was believed at the time to be <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a>. In subsequent years, he was plagued with what was thought to be <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a> and other fevers, bleeding from the eyes, temporary blindness and prolonged attacks of gout. The attacks increased in duration and severity, sometimes leaving Columbus bedridden for months at a time, and culminated in his death 14 years later.
</p><p>Based on Columbus's lifestyle and the described symptoms, some modern commentators suspect that he suffered from <a href="/wiki/Reactive_arthritis" title="Reactive arthritis">reactive arthritis</a>, rather than gout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMD_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-UMD-208">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoenig_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoenig-209">[198]</a></sup> Reactive arthritis is a joint inflammation caused by intestinal bacterial infections or after acquiring certain sexually transmitted diseases (primarily <a href="/wiki/Chlamydia" title="Chlamydia">chlamydia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gonorrhea" title="Gonorrhea">gonorrhea</a>). In 2006,  Frank C. Arnett, a medical doctor, and historian Charles Merrill, published their paper in <i>The American Journal of the Medical Sciences</i> proposing that Columbus had a form of reactive arthritis; Merrill made the case in that same paper that Columbus was the son of Catalans and his mother possibly a member of a prominent <i><a href="/wiki/Converso" title="Converso">converso</a></i> (converted Jew) family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArnettMerrill2006_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArnettMerrill2006-210">[199]</a></sup> "It seems likely that [Columbus] acquired reactive arthritis from food poisoning on one of his ocean voyages because of poor sanitation and improper food preparation," says Arnett, a <a href="/wiki/Rheumatology" title="Rheumatology">rheumatologist</a> and professor of internal medicine, pathology and laboratory medicine at the University of Texas Medical School at Houston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMD_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-UMD-208">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some historians such as H. Micheal Tarver and Emily Slape,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TarverSlape2016_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-TarverSlape2016-211">[200]</a></sup> as well as medical doctors such as Arnett and Antonio Rodríguez Cuartero,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElUniversal2007_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElUniversal2007-212">[201]</a></sup> believe that Columbus had such a form of reactive arthritis, but according to other authorities, this is "speculative",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScottGalloway2015_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScottGalloway2015-213">[202]</a></sup> or "very speculative".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RitchlinFitzGerald2007_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-RitchlinFitzGerald2007-214">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>After his arrival to Sanlúcar from his fourth voyage (and Queen Isabella's death), an ill Columbus settled in Seville in April 1505. He stubbornly continued to make pleas to the Crown to defend his own personal privileges and his family's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECuartero_y_Huerta198874_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECuartero_y_Huerta198874-215">[204]</a></sup> He moved to <a href="/wiki/Segovia" title="Segovia">Segovia</a> (where the court was at the time) on a mule by early 1506,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kadir1992193_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kadir1992193-216">[205]</a></sup> and, on the occasion of the wedding of King Ferdinand with <a href="/wiki/Germaine_of_Foix" title="Germaine of Foix">Germaine of Foix</a> in <a href="/wiki/Valladolid" title="Valladolid">Valladolid</a>, Spain, in March 1506, Columbus moved to that city to persist with his demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[206]</a></sup> On 20 May 1506, aged 54, Columbus died in Valladolid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991194_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991194-218">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_of_remains">Location of remains</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:312px;max-width:312px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:151px;max-width:151px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:206px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2261" data-file-width="1627" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg/149px-Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg/224px-Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg/298px-Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg 2x" width="149"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Tomb in <a href="/wiki/Seville_Cathedral" title="Seville Cathedral">Seville Cathedral</a>. The remains in the casket are borne by kings of Castile, Leon, Aragon, and Navarre</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:157px;max-width:157px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:206px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_Tomb_Dominican_Republic_01_2018_6805.jpg"><img alt="A large white, black, and gold tomb elaborately adorned with sculpture and writing, claiming to be the resting place of Cristobal Colon" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2250" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Columbus_Tomb_Dominican_Republic_01_2018_6805.jpg/155px-Columbus_Tomb_Dominican_Republic_01_2018_6805.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Columbus_Tomb_Dominican_Republic_01_2018_6805.jpg/233px-Columbus_Tomb_Dominican_Republic_01_2018_6805.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Columbus_Tomb_Dominican_Republic_01_2018_6805.jpg/310px-Columbus_Tomb_Dominican_Republic_01_2018_6805.jpg 2x" width="155"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Tomb in <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Lighthouse" title="Columbus Lighthouse">Columbus Lighthouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo_Este" title="Santo Domingo Este">Santo Domingo Este</a>, Dominican Republic</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Columbus's remains were first buried at a convent in Valladolid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991196_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991196-219">[208]</a></sup> then moved to the <a href="/wiki/Monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_las_Cuevas" title="Monastery of Santa Maria de las Cuevas">monastery of La Cartuja</a> in Seville (southern Spain) by the will of his son Diego.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nash2005_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nash2005-220">[209]</a></sup> They may have been exhumed in 1513 and interred at the <a href="/wiki/Seville_Cathedral" title="Seville Cathedral">Seville Cathedral</a>. In about 1536, the remains of both Columbus and his son Diego were moved to a cathedral in <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_Colonial_(Santo_Domingo)" title="Ciudad Colonial (Santo Domingo)">Colonial Santo Domingo</a>, in the present-day <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>; Columbus had requested to be buried on the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian2003_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2003-221">[210]</a></sup> By some accounts, in 1793, when France took over the entire island of Hispaniola, Columbus's remains were moved to <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, Cuba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElPaís2021_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElPaís2021-222">[211]</a></sup> After Cuba became independent following the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a> in 1898, at least some of these remains were moved back to the Seville Cathedral,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991196_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991196-219">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNA_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-223">[212]</a></sup> where they were placed on an elaborate <a href="/wiki/Catafalque" title="Catafalque">catafalque</a>.
</p><p>In June 2003, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> samples were taken from these remains<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian2003_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2003-221">[210]</a></sup> as well as those of Columbus's brother Diego and younger son Fernando. Initial observations suggested that the bones did not appear to match Columbus's physique or age at death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[213]</a></sup> DNA extraction proved difficult; only short fragments of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> could be isolated. These matched corresponding DNA from Columbus's brother, supporting that both individuals had shared the same mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNA_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-223">[212]</a></sup> Such evidence, together with <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologic</a> and historic analyses, led the researchers to conclude that the remains belonged to Christopher Columbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ÁlvarezMartinez2010_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-ÁlvarezMartinez2010-225">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ancestry_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-ancestry-227">[l]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1877, a priest discovered a lead box at Santo Domingo inscribed: "Discoverer of America, First Admiral". Inscriptions found the next year read "Last of the remains of the first admiral, Sire Christopher Columbus, discoverer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011363–64_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011363–64-228">[216]</a></sup> The box contained bones of an arm and a leg, as well as a bullet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[m]</a></sup> These remains were considered legitimate by physician and U.S. Assistant Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_Eugene_Osborne" title="John Eugene Osborne">John Eugene Osborne</a>, who suggested in 1913 that they travel through the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a> as a part of its opening ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EveStar_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-EveStar-231">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[n]</a></sup> These remains were kept at the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_Cathedral_of_Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_la_Menor" title="Basilica Cathedral of Santa María la Menor">Basilica Cathedral of Santa María la Menor</a> (in the Colonial City of Santo Domingo) before being moved to the <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Lighthouse" title="Columbus Lighthouse">Columbus Lighthouse</a> (<a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo_Este" title="Santo Domingo Este">Santo Domingo Este</a>, inaugurated in 1992). The authorities in Santo Domingo have never allowed these remains to be DNA-tested, so it is unconfirmed whether they are from Columbus's body as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNA_223-2"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-223">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bare_url_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-bare_url-233">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[o]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_places_named_for_Christopher_Columbus" title="List of places named for Christopher Columbus">List of places named for Christopher Columbus</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_monuments_and_memorials_to_Christopher_Columbus" title="List of monuments and memorials to Christopher Columbus">List of monuments and memorials to Christopher Columbus</a></div>
<p>The figure of Columbus was not ignored in the British colonies during the colonial era: Columbus became a unifying symbol early in the history of the colonies that became the United States when Puritan preachers began to use his life story as a model for a "developing American spirit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West1992_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-West1992-236">[221]</a></sup> In the spring of 1692, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> preacher <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Mather" title="Cotton Mather">Cotton Mather</a> described Columbus's voyage as one of three shaping events of the modern age, connecting Columbus's voyage and the Puritans' migration to North America, seeing them together as the key to a grand design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bercovitch2014_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bercovitch2014-237">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of Columbus as a founding figure of New World nations spread rapidly after the American Revolution. This was out of a desire to develop a national history and <a href="/wiki/Origin_myth" title="Origin myth">founding myth</a> with fewer ties to Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bushman1992_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bushman1992-238">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartosik-Vélez20142_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartosik-Vélez20142-239">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burmila20171009_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burmila20171009-240">[225]</a></sup> His name was the basis for the female <a href="/wiki/National_personification" title="National personification">national personification</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(personification)" title="Columbia (personification)">Columbia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dewey2007_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dewey2007-241">[226]</a></sup> in use since the 1730s with reference to the original <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a>, and also a historical name applied to the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> and to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>. The federal capital (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a>) was named for her,  as well as <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_South_Carolina" title="Columbia, South Carolina">Columbia, South Carolina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Rediviva" title="Columbia Rediviva">Columbia Rediviva</a>, the ship for which the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_River" title="Columbia River">Columbia River</a> was named.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benke2011_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benke2011-242">[227]</a></sup>
</p><p>Columbus's name was given to the newly born <a href="/wiki/Gran_Colombia" title="Gran Colombia">Republic of Colombia</a> in the early 19th century, inspired by the political project of "Colombeia" developed by revolutionary <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Miranda" title="Francisco de Miranda">Francisco de Miranda</a>, which was put at the service of the emancipation of continental Hispanic America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1212" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg/220px-1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg/330px-1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg/440px-1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replicas of <i>Niña</i>, <i>Pinta</i> and <i>Santa María</i> sailed from Spain to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicago_Columbian_Exposition" title="Chicago Columbian Exposition">Chicago Columbian Exposition</a> in 1893</div></div></div>
<p>To commemorate the 400th anniversary of the landing of Columbus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-244">[229]</a></sup> the 1893 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World%27s_Fair" title="World's Fair">World's Fair</a> in Chicago was named the <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Columbian_Exposition" title="World's Columbian Exposition">World's Columbian Exposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BolotinLaing2002_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-BolotinLaing2002-245">[230]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Postal_Service" title="U.S. Postal Service">U.S. Postal Service</a> issued the first U.S. <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_stamp" title="Commemorative stamp">commemorative stamps</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Issue" title="Columbian Issue">Columbian Issue</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handler2016_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Handler2016-246">[231]</a></sup> depicting Columbus, Queen Isabella and others in various stages of his several voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West2014_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-West2014-247">[232]</a></sup> The policies related to the celebration of the Spanish colonial empire as the vehicle of a nationalist project undertaken in Spain during the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Spain)" title="Restoration (Spain)">Restoration</a> in the late 19th century took form with the commemoration of the 4th centenary on 12 October 1892 (in which the figure of Columbus was extolled by the Conservative government), eventually becoming the very same national day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarcilhacy2011135–138_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarcilhacy2011135–138-248">[233]</a></sup> Several monuments commemorating the "discovery" were erected in cities such as Palos, Barcelona, Granada, Madrid, Salamanca, Valladolid and Seville in the years around the 400th anniversary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[p]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Quincentenary" title="Columbus Quincentenary">Columbus Quincentenary</a> in 1992, a second Columbian issue was released jointly with Italy, Portugal, and Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[235]</a></sup> Columbus was celebrated at <a href="/wiki/Seville_Expo_%2792" title="Seville Expo '92">Seville Expo '92</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genoa_Expo_%2792" title="Genoa Expo '92">Genoa Expo '92</a>.
</p><p>The Boal Mansion Museum, founded in 1951, contains a collection of materials concerning later descendants of Columbus and collateral branches of the family. It features a 16th-century chapel from a Spanish castle reputedly owned by Diego Colón which became the residence of Columbus's descendants. The chapel interior was dismantled and moved from Spain in 1909 and re-erected on the Boal estate at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boalsburg" title="Boalsburg">Boalsburg</a>, Pennsylvania. Inside it are numerous religious paintings and other objects including a <a href="/wiki/Reliquary" title="Reliquary">reliquary</a> with fragments of wood supposedly from the <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">True Cross</a>. The museum also holds a collection of documents mostly relating to Columbus descendants of the late 18th and early 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedini2016489_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bedini2016489-252">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many countries of the Americas, as well as Spain and Italy, <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Day" title="Columbus Day">Columbus Day</a> celebrates the anniversary of Columbus's arrival in the Americas on 12 October 1492.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[237]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<p>The voyages of Columbus are considered a turning point in human history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BelloShaver2011_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-BelloShaver2011-254">[238]</a></sup> marking the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a> and accompanying demographic, commercial, economic, social, and political changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[239]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Landing_of_Columbus_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1971" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg/220px-Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg/330px-Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg/440px-Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Landing_of_Columbus_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Landing of Columbus at the Island of Guanahaní, West Indies</i> (1846), by <a href="/wiki/John_Vanderlyn" title="John Vanderlyn">John Vanderlyn</a>. The landing of Columbus became a powerful icon of American genesis in the 19th century.</div></div></div>
<p>His explorations resulted in permanent contact between the two hemispheres, and the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a>" is used to refer to the cultures of the Americas before the arrival of Columbus and his European successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFarlane2004_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-McFarlane2004-256">[240]</a></sup> The ensuing Columbian exchange saw the massive <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">exchange</a> of animals, plants, fungi, diseases, technologies, mineral wealth and ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[241]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first century after his endeavors, Columbus's figure largely languished in the backwaters of history, and his reputation was beset by his failures as a colonial administrator. His legacy was somewhat rescued from oblivion when he began to appear as a character in Italian and Spanish plays and poems from the late 16th century onward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noble_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-noble-258">[242]</a></sup>
</p><p>Columbus was subsumed into the Western narrative of colonization and empire building, which invoked notions of <i><a href="/wiki/Translatio_imperii" title="Translatio imperii">translatio imperii</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Translatio_studii" title="Translatio studii">translatio studii</a></i> to underline who was considered "civilized" and who was not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartosik-Vélez2014_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartosik-Vélez2014-259">[243]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Discovery-statue.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1865" data-file-width="1079" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Discovery-statue.JPG/170px-Discovery-statue.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Discovery-statue.JPG/255px-Discovery-statue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Discovery-statue.JPG/340px-Discovery-statue.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Discovery-statue.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Discovery_of_America_(sculpture)" title="The Discovery of America (sculpture)">The Discovery of America</a></i> sculpture, depicting Columbus and a cowering Indian maiden, stood outside the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Capitol" title="U.S. Capitol">U.S. Capitol</a> from 1844 to 1958.</div></div></div>
<p>The Americanization of the figure of Columbus began in the latter decades of the 18th century, after the revolutionary period of the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heike2014_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heike2014-260">[244]</a></sup> elevating the status of his reputation to a national myth, <i>homo americanus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul201458,_60_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul201458,_60-261">[245]</a></sup> His landing became a powerful icon as an "image of American genesis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heike2014_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heike2014-260">[244]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Discovery_of_America_(sculpture)" title="The Discovery of America (sculpture)">The Discovery of America</a></i> sculpture, depicting Columbus and a cowering Indian maiden, was commissioned on April 3, 1837, when U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> sanctioned the engineering of <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Persico" title="Luigi Persico">Luigi Persico</a>’s design. This representation of Columbus's triumph and the Indian's recoil is a demonstration of white superiority over savage, naive Indians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryd2001_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fryd2001-262">[246]</a></sup> As recorded during its unveiling in 1844, the sculpture extends to "represent the meeting of the two races", as Persico captures their first interaction, highlighting the "moral and intellectual inferiority" of Indians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[247]</a></sup> Placed outside the U.S. Capitol building where it remained until its removal in the mid-20th century, the sculpture reflected the contemporary view of whites in the U.S. toward the Natives; they are labeled "merciless Indian savages" in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[248]</a></sup> In 1836, Pennsylvania senator and future U.S. President <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>, who proposed the sculpture, described it as representing "the great discoverer when he first bounded with ecstasy upon the shore, ail his toils past, presenting a hemisphere to the astonished world, with the name America inscribed upon it. Whilst he is thus standing upon the shore, a female savage, with awe and wonder depicted in her countenance, is gazing upon him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[249]</a></sup>
</p><p>The American Columbus myth was reconfigured later in the century when he was enlisted as an ethnic hero by immigrants to the United States who were not of Anglo-Saxon stock, such as Jewish, Italian, and Irish people, who claimed Columbus as a sort of ethnic founding father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul201463–64_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul201463–64-266">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dennis2018_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dennis2018-267">[251]</a></sup> Catholics unsuccessfully tried to promote him for <a href="/wiki/Canonization" title="Canonization">canonization</a> in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilford199180_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilford199180-268">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[253]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the 1990s onward, a narrative of Columbus being responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Genocide_of_indigenous_peoples" title="Genocide of indigenous peoples">genocide of indigenous peoples</a> and environmental destruction began to compete with the then predominant discourse of Columbus as Christ-bearer, scientist, or father of America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[254]</a></sup> This narrative features the negative effects of Columbus' conquests on native populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stannard1993_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stannard1993-151">[142]</a></sup> Exposed to <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> diseases, the <a href="/wiki/Population_history_of_Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Population history of Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous populations of the New World</a> collapsed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Axtell1992_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Axtell1992-271">[255]</a></sup> and were largely replaced by Europeans and Africans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houbert2003_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houbert2003-272">[256]</a></sup> who brought with them new methods of farming, business, governance, and religious worship.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Originality_of_discovery_of_America">Originality of discovery of America</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories" title="Pre-Columbian trans-oceanic contact theories">Pre-Columbian trans-oceanic contact theories</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_North_America" title="Norse colonization of North America">Norse colonization of North America</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamps_225-226_Discovery_of_America.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="1030" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Faroe_stamps_225-226_Discovery_of_America.jpg/220px-Faroe_stamps_225-226_Discovery_of_America.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Faroe_stamps_225-226_Discovery_of_America.jpg/330px-Faroe_stamps_225-226_Discovery_of_America.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Faroe_stamps_225-226_Discovery_of_America.jpg/440px-Faroe_stamps_225-226_Discovery_of_America.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamps_225-226_Discovery_of_America.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Discovery of America</i>, a postage stamp from the Faroe Islands commemorates the voyages of discovery of <a href="/wiki/Leif_Erikson" title="Leif Erikson">Leif Erikson</a> (<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1000</span>) and Christopher Columbus (1492)</div></div></div>
<p>Though Christopher Columbus came to be considered the European discoverer of America in Western popular culture, his historical legacy is more nuanced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2000_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2000-273">[257]</a></sup> After settling Iceland, the <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> settled the uninhabited southern part of <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> beginning in the 10th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nedkvitne2018_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nedkvitne2018-274">[258]</a></sup> Norsemen are believed to have then set sail from Greenland and Iceland to become the first known Europeans to reach the North American mainland, nearly 500 years before Columbus reached the Caribbean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-275">[259]</a></sup> The 1960s discovery of a Norse settlement dating to c. 1000 AD at <a href="/wiki/L%27Anse_aux_Meadows" title="L'Anse aux Meadows">L'Anse aux Meadows</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newfoundland" title="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</a>, partially corroborates accounts within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Icelandic_sagas" title="Icelandic sagas">Icelandic sagas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Erik_the_Red" title="Erik the Red">Erik the Red</a>'s colonization of Greenland and his son <a href="/wiki/Leif_Erikson" title="Leif Erikson">Leif Erikson</a>'s subsequent exploration of a place he called <a href="/wiki/Vinland" title="Vinland">Vinland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-276">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 19th century, amid <a href="/wiki/Viking_revival" title="Viking revival">a revival of interest in Norse culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Christian_Rafn" title="Carl Christian Rafn">Carl Christian Rafn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_DeCosta" title="Benjamin Franklin DeCosta">Benjamin Franklin DeCosta</a> wrote works establishing that the Norse had preceded Columbus in colonizing the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-278">[262]</a></sup> Following this, in 1874 <a href="/wiki/Rasmus_B._Anderson" title="Rasmus B. Anderson">Rasmus Bjørn Anderson</a> argued that Columbus must have known of the North American continent before he started his voyage of discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolodny2012_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kolodny2012-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-275">[259]</a></sup> Most modern scholars doubt Columbus had knowledge of the Norse settlements in America, with his arrival to the continent being most likely an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_discovery" title="Independent discovery">independent discovery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enterline2003_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Enterline2003-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PaolucciPaolucci1992_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-PaolucciPaolucci1992-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolodny2012_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kolodny2012-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Restall2021_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-Restall2021-279">[263]</a></sup>
</p><p>Europeans devised explanations for the <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">origins of the Native Americans</a> and their geographical distribution with narratives that often served to reinforce their own <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Preconception&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Preconception (page does not exist)">preconceptions</a> built on ancient intellectual foundations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berkhofer1979_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berkhofer1979-280">[264]</a></sup> In modern Latin America, the non-Native populations of some countries often demonstrate an ambiguous attitude toward the perspectives of indigenous peoples regarding the so-called "discovery" by Columbus and the era of <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> that followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coronil1989_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coronil1989-281">[265]</a></sup>
In his 1960 <a href="/wiki/Monograph" title="Monograph">monograph</a>, Mexican philosopher and historian <a href="/wiki/Edmundo_O%27Gorman" title="Edmundo O'Gorman">Edmundo O'Gorman</a> explicitly rejects the Columbus discovery myth, arguing that the idea that Columbus discovered America was a misleading legend fixed in the public mind through the works of American author <a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a> during the 19th century. O'Gorman argues that to assert Columbus "discovered America" is to shape the facts concerning the events of 1492 to make them conform to an interpretation that arose many years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuccetelli2020_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nuccetelli2020-282">[266]</a></sup> For him, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurocentric" title="Eurocentric">Eurocentric</a> view of the discovery of America sustains systems of domination in ways that favor Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lazo2013_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lazo2013-283">[267]</a></sup>
In a 1992 article for <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_UNESCO_Courier" title="The UNESCO Courier">The UNESCO Courier</a></i>, Félix Fernández-Shaw argues that the word "discovery" prioritizes European explorers as the "heroes" of the contact between the Old and New World. He suggests that the word "encounter" is more appropriate, being a more universal term which includes Native Americans in the narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernández-Shaw1992_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernández-Shaw1992-284">[268]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="America_as_a_distinct_land">America as a distinct land</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_Monument_(New_York_City)_-_DSC05924.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4320" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Columbus_Monument_%28New_York_City%29_-_DSC05924.JPG/220px-Columbus_Monument_%28New_York_City%29_-_DSC05924.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Columbus_Monument_%28New_York_City%29_-_DSC05924.JPG/330px-Columbus_Monument_%28New_York_City%29_-_DSC05924.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Columbus_Monument_%28New_York_City%29_-_DSC05924.JPG/440px-Columbus_Monument_%28New_York_City%29_-_DSC05924.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_Monument_(New_York_City)_-_DSC05924.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Monument_(New_York_City)" title="Columbus Monument (New York City)">Columbus Monument</a> in <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Circle" title="Columbus Circle">Columbus Circle</a>, New York City</div></div></div>
<p>Historians have traditionally argued that Columbus remained convinced until his death that his journeys had been along the east coast of Asia as he originally intended<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burmila20171009_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burmila20171009-240">[225]</a></sup> (excluding arguments such as Anderson's).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolodny2012_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kolodny2012-33">[31]</a></sup> On his third voyage he briefly referred to South America as a "hitherto unknown" continent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-incognita_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-incognita-99">[e]</a></sup> while also rationalizing that it was the "Earthly Paradise" located "at the end of the Orient".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeruvabel2003_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zeruvabel2003-164">[154]</a></sup> Columbus continued to claim in his later writings that he had reached Asia; in a 1502 letter to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a>, he asserts that Cuba is the east coast of Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992227_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsPhillips1992227-46">[44]</a></sup> On the other hand, in a document in the <i>Book of Privileges</i> (1502), Columbus refers to the New World as the <i>Indias Occidentales</i> ('West Indies'), which he says "were unknown to all the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[270]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shape_of_the_Earth">Shape of the Earth</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Myth_of_the_flat_Earth" title="Myth of the flat Earth">Myth of the flat Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faro_colon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Faro_colon.jpg/220px-Faro_colon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Faro_colon.jpg/330px-Faro_colon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Faro_colon.jpg/440px-Faro_colon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faro_colon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Columbus_Lighthouse" title="Columbus Lighthouse">Columbus Lighthouse</a>, a Museum and <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">Mausoleum</a> in homage to Christopher Columbus in <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Washington Irving's 1828 biography of Columbus popularized the idea that Columbus had difficulty obtaining support for his plan because many Catholic theologians insisted that the <a href="/wiki/Flat_Earth" title="Flat Earth">Earth was flat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book3_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-book3-287">[271]</a></sup> but this is a popular misconception which can be traced back to 17th-century Protestants campaigning against Catholicism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[272]</a></sup> In fact, the spherical shape of the Earth had been known to scholars since antiquity, and was common knowledge among sailors, including Columbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011244_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2011244-289">[273]</a></sup> Coincidentally, the oldest surviving globe of the Earth, the <a href="/wiki/Erdapfel" title="Erdapfel">Erdapfel</a>, was made in 1492, just before Columbus's return to Europe from his first voyage. As such it contains no sign of the Americas and yet demonstrates the common belief in a spherical Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>Making observations with a <a href="/wiki/Quadrant_(instrument)" title="Quadrant (instrument)">quadrant</a> on his third voyage, Columbus inaccurately measured the <a href="/wiki/Polar_distance_(astronomy)" title="Polar distance (astronomy)">polar radius</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Star" title="North Star">North Star</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Diurnal_motion" title="Diurnal motion">diurnal motion</a> to be five degrees, double the value of another erroneous reading he had made from further north. This led him to describe the <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth#Pear_shape" title="Figure of the Earth">figure of the Earth as pear-shaped</a>, with the "stalk" portion ascending towards Heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991557_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991557-291">[275]</a></sup> In fact, the Earth ever so slightly is pear-shaped, with its "stalk" pointing north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[276]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_and_defense">Criticism and defense</span></h3>
<p>Columbus is criticized both for his brutality and for initiating the depopulation of the indigenous peoples of the Caribbean, whether by imported diseases or intentional violence. According to scholars of Native American history, <a href="/wiki/George_Tinker" title="George Tinker">George Tinker</a> and Mark Freedman, Columbus was responsible for creating a cycle of "murder, violence, and slavery" to maximize exploitation of the Caribbean islands' resources, and that Native deaths on the scale at which they occurred would not have been caused by new diseases alone. Further, they describe the proposition that disease and not genocide caused these deaths as "American <a href="/wiki/Holocaust_denial" title="Holocaust denial">holocaust denial</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TinkerFreeland2008_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-TinkerFreeland2008-293">[277]</a></sup> Other scholars defend Columbus's actions or allege that the worst accusations against him are not based in fact while others claim that "he has been blamed for events far beyond his own reach or knowledge".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-294">[278]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of the <a href="/wiki/George_Floyd_protests" title="George Floyd protests">protests and riots</a> that followed the <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_George_Floyd" title="Murder of George Floyd">murder of George Floyd</a> in 2020, many public <a href="/wiki/List_of_monuments_and_memorials_removed_during_the_George_Floyd_protests#Christopher_Columbus" title="List of monuments and memorials removed during the George Floyd protests">monuments of Christopher Columbus have been removed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[279]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brutality">Brutality</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pedestal_base_of_Christopher_Columbus_statue_2.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Pedestal_base_of_Christopher_Columbus_statue_2.jpeg/220px-Pedestal_base_of_Christopher_Columbus_statue_2.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Pedestal_base_of_Christopher_Columbus_statue_2.jpeg/330px-Pedestal_base_of_Christopher_Columbus_statue_2.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Pedestal_base_of_Christopher_Columbus_statue_2.jpeg/440px-Pedestal_base_of_Christopher_Columbus_statue_2.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pedestal_base_of_Christopher_Columbus_statue_2.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The remains of the pedestal base of the Columbus statue in the Baltimore inner harbor area. The statue was thrown into the harbor on 4 July 2020, as part of the <a href="/wiki/George_Floyd_protests" title="George Floyd protests">George Floyd protests</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some historians have criticized Columbus for initiating the widespread colonization of the Americas and for abusing its native population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_116-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-116">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[281]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[282]</a></sup> On St. Croix<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (September 2022)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, Columbus's friend Michele da Cuneo—according to his own account—kept an indigenous woman he captured, whom Columbus "gave to [him]", then brutally raped her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorison1991417_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorison1991417-299">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[q]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[r]</a></sup> The punishment for an indigenous person, aged 14 and older, failing to pay a hawk's bell, or <i>cascabela</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeaganCruxent2002_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeaganCruxent2002-304">[286]</a></sup> worth of gold dust every six months (based on <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de las Casas">Bartolomé de las Casas</a>'s account) was cutting off the hands of those without tokens, often leaving them to bleed to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TinkerFreeland2008_293-1"><a href="#cite_note-TinkerFreeland2008-293">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_116-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-116">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koning_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koning-305">[287]</a></sup> Columbus had an economic interest in the enslavement of the Hispaniola natives and for that reason was not eager to baptize them, which attracted criticism from some churchmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varela_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-varela-306">[288]</a></sup> Consuelo Varela, a Spanish historian who has seen Bobadilla's report, states that "Columbus's government was characterized by a form of tyranny. Even those who loved him had to admit the atrocities that had taken place."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newspaper1_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-newspaper1-173">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kris_Lane" title="Kris Lane">Kris Lane</a> disputes whether it is appropriate to use the term "genocide" when the atrocities were not Columbus's intent, but resulted from his decrees, family business goals, and negligence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-148">[139]</a></sup> Other historians have argued that some of the accounts of the brutality of Columbus and his brothers have been exaggerated as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_legend_(Spain)" title="Black legend (Spain)">Black Legend</a>, a historical tendency towards anti-Spanish sentiment in historical sources dating as far back as the 16th century, which they speculate may continue to taint scholarship into the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to historian Emily Berquist Soule, the immense Portuguese profits from the maritime trade in African slaves along the West African coast served as an inspiration for Columbus to create a counterpart of this apparatus in the New World using indigenous American slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[292]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/William_J._Connell_(historian)" title="William J. Connell (historian)">William J. Connell</a> has argued that while Columbus "brought the entrepreneurial form of slavery to the New World," this "was a phenomenon of the times," further arguing that "we have to be very careful about applying 20th-century understandings of morality to the morality of the 15th century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[293]</a></sup> In a less popular defense of colonization, Spanish ambassador María Jesús Figa López-Palop has argued, "Normally we melded with the cultures in America, we stayed there, we spread our language and culture and religion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorwitz200884_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorwitz200884-312">[294]</a></sup>
</p><p>British historian <a href="/wiki/Basil_Davidson" title="Basil Davidson">Basil Davidson</a> has dubbed Columbus the "father of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">slave trade</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[295]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[296]</a></sup> citing the fact that the first license to ship enslaved Africans to the Caribbean was issued by the Catholic Monarchs in 1501 to the first royal governor of Hispaniola, <a href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_de_Ovando" title="Nicolás de Ovando">Nicolás de Ovando</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jennings2020_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jennings2020-315">[297]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Depopulation">Depopulation</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taino#Depopulation" title="Taino">Taino § Depopulation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_history_of_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Population history of indigenous peoples of the Americas">Population history of indigenous peoples of the Americas</a></div>
<p>Around the turn of the 21st century, estimates for the <span class="nowrap">pre-Columbian</span> population of Hispaniola ranged between 250,000 and two million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991183,_190_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991183,_190-155">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZinn20035_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZinn20035-316">[298]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keegan_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keegan-317">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[s]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Genetic_analysis" title="Genetic analysis">genetic analysis</a> published in late 2020 suggests that smaller figures are more likely, perhaps as low as 10,000–50,000 for Hispaniola and Puerto Rico combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernandes_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes-319">[300]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[301]</a></sup> Based on the previous figures of a few hundred thousand, some have estimated that a third or more of the natives in Haiti were dead within the first two years of Columbus's governorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_116-5"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-116">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991183,_190_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991183,_190-155">[145]</a></sup> Contributors to depopulation included disease, warfare, and harsh enslavement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorwitz2008_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorwitz2008-321">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[303]</a></sup> Indirect evidence suggests that some serious illness may have arrived with the 1,500 colonists who accompanied Columbus' second expedition in 1493.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorwitz2008_321-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorwitz2008-321">[302]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_C._Mann" title="Charles C. Mann">Charles C. Mann</a> writes that "It was as if the suffering these diseases had caused in <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> over the past millennia were concentrated into the span of decades."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[304]</a></sup> A third of the natives forced to work in gold and silver mines died every six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hickel_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hickel-324">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-325">[306]</a></sup> Within three to six decades, the surviving Arawak population numbered only in the hundreds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hickel_324-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hickel-324">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson1991183,_190_155-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1991183,_190-155">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[307]</a></sup> The indigenous population of the Americas overall is thought to have been reduced by about 90% in the century after Columbus's arrival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[308]</a></sup> Among indigenous peoples, Columbus is often viewed as a key agent of genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[309]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Eliot_Morison" title="Samuel Eliot Morison">Samuel Eliot Morison</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvard" title="Harvard">Harvard</a> historian and author of a multivolume biography on Columbus, writes, "The cruel policy initiated by Columbus and pursued by his successors resulted in complete genocide."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morison_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morison-329">[310]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Noble David Cook, "There were too few Spaniards to have killed the millions who were reported to have died in the first century after Old and New World contact." He instead estimates that the death toll was caused by <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook1998_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook1998-330">[311]</a></sup> which may have caused a pandemic only after the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a> in 1519.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[312]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[313]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[314]</a></sup> According to some estimates, smallpox had an 80–90% fatality rate in Native American populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[315]</a></sup> The natives had no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acquired_immunity" title="Acquired immunity">acquired immunity</a> to these new diseases and suffered high fatalities. There is also evidence that they had poor diets and were overworked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Austin-Alchon2003_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-Alchon2003-145">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[317]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Res%C3%A9ndez" title="Andrés Reséndez">Andrés Reséndez</a> of <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Davis" title="University of California, Davis">University of California, Davis</a>, says the available evidence suggests "slavery has emerged as major killer" of the indigenous populations of the Caribbean between 1492 and 1550 more so than diseases such as smallpox, influenza and malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[318]</a></sup> He says that indigenous populations did not experience a rebound like European populations did following the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> because unlike the latter, a large portion of the former were subjected to deadly forced labor in the mines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_325-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-325">[306]</a></sup>
